The work of a slave of the holy place, Maqsud of Kashan in the year 926" (the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic</a> year corresponding to 1520).<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_179_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_179-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the restoration conducted in the late 1980s, Quinlan Terry restored the fireplace. Executed in the Kentian style, the small Ionic pilasters in the over-mantel are miniature duplicates of the large Ionic pillars in the room. The Ionic motif is also found in the door surrounds and panelling.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sparsely furnished with a few chairs and sofas around the walls, the Pillared Room is usually used to receive guests before they go into the State Dining Room. However, it is sometimes used for other purposes that require a large open space. International agreements have been signed in this room. Tony Blair entertained the <a href="/wiki/England_national_rugby_union_team" title="England national rugby union team">England Rugby Union team</a> in the Pillared Room after they won the <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">World Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/2003_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2003 Rugby World Cup">2003</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Logie_Baird" title="John Logie Baird">John Logie Baird</a> gave <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> a demonstration of his invention, the television, in this room.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <i>(See The Pillared Drawing Room c1927<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>)</i><sup id="cite_ref-Plate126_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plate126-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Terracotta_State_Drawing_Room">Terracotta State Drawing Room</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Terracotta State Drawing Room">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Terracotta Room is the middle of the three drawing rooms. It was used as the dining room when Sir Robert Walpole was Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The name changes according to the colour it is painted. When Margaret Thatcher came to power it was the Blue Room; she had it re-decorated and renamed the Green Room. It is now painted terracotta.\n</p><p>In the renovation of the 1980s Quinlan Terry introduced large Doric order columns to this room in the door surrounds and designed a very large <a href="/wiki/Palladian_architecture" title="Palladian architecture">Palladian</a> overmantel for the fireplace with small double Doric columns on each side with the royal arms above. Terry also added an ornate gilded ceiling to give the rooms a more stately look. Carved into the plasterwork above the door leading to the Pillared Room is a tribute to Margaret Thatcher: a straw-carrying \'thatcher\'.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="White_State_Drawing_Room">White State Drawing Room</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: White State Drawing Room">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_(28305052402).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg/220px-Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg/330px-Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg/440px-Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_%2828305052402%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4819" data-file-height="3212" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Secretary_Kerry_Sits_With_British_Prime_Minister_May_in_the_White_Room_No._10_Downing_Street_in_London_(28305052402).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> talking with <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> in the White State Drawing Room</div></div></div>\n<p>The White State Drawing room was, until the 1940s, used by Prime Ministers and their partners for their private use. It was here that <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> kept his grand piano. It is often used as the backdrop for television interviews and is in regular use as a meeting room for Downing Street staff. The room links through to the Terracotta Room next door. In the reconstruction during the late 1980s, Quinlan Terry used Corinthian columns and added ornate Baroque-style central ceiling mouldings and corner mouldings of the four national flowers of the United Kingdom: rose (England), thistle (Scotland), daffodil (Wales) and shamrock (Northern Ireland).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_Dining_Room">State Dining Room</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: State Dining Room">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When <a href="/wiki/Frederick_John_Robinson,_1st_Viscount_Goderich" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick John Robinson, 1st Viscount Goderich">Frederick Robinson</a> (later Lord Goderich), became Chancellor of the Exchequer in 1823, he decided to leave a personal legacy to the nation. To this end, he employed <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Soane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Soane">Sir John Soane</a>, the distinguished architect who had designed the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> and many other famous buildings, to build a State Dining Room for Number 10. Begun in 1825 and completed in 1826 at a cost of \xc2\xa32,000, the result is a spacious room with oak panelling and reeded mouldings. Accessed through the first floor, its vaulted, arched ceiling rises up through the next so that it actually occupies two floors. Measuring 42 feet (13&#160;m) by 26 feet (7.9&#160;m), it is the largest room in Number 10. Soane was the guest of honour when the dining room was first used on 4 April 1826.\n</p><p>The room is usually furnished with a table surrounded by 20 reproduction <a href="/wiki/Adam_style" title="Adam style">Adam style</a> chairs originally made for the British Embassy in Rio de Janeiro. For larger gatherings, a horseshoe-shaped table is brought in that will accommodate up to 65 guests. On these occasions, the table is set with the Silver Trust Silver set given to Downing Street in the 1990s. (See the State Dining room with the Silver Trust Silver in use for a luncheon<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>) Above the fireplace, overlooking the room, is a massive portrait by <a href="/wiki/John_Shackleton" title="John Shackleton">John Shackleton</a> of George II, the king who originally gave the building to the First Lord of the Treasury in 1732.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_180_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_180-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Celebrity chefs such as Nigella Lawson have cooked for Prime Ministers\' guests using the small kitchen next door. Entering through the Small Dining Room, Blair used this room for his monthly press conferences.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <i>(See Simon Schama\'s Tour of Downing Street. Pt 3: The Dining Room<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></i>\n(See The State Dining Room c1930: View toward the entrance<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and View from the entrance<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and also a more modern view<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>)<i><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup></i>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_kitchen">Great kitchen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Great kitchen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:No_10,_Downing_Street,_Kitchen_1931.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png/220px-No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png/330px-No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png/440px-No_10%2C_Downing_Street%2C_Kitchen_1931.png 2x" data-file-width="669" data-file-height="831" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:No_10,_Downing_Street,_Kitchen_1931.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Great kitchen, 1931</div></div></div>\n<p>The great kitchen located in the basement was another part of the renovations begun in 1783, probably also under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Taylor_(architect)" title="Robert Taylor (architect)">Robert Taylor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-multiref6_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multiref6-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Seldom seen by anyone other than staff, the space is two storeys high with a huge arched window and vaulted ceiling. Traditionally, it has always had a chopping block work table in the centre that is 14 feet (4.3&#160;m) long, 3 feet (0.91&#160;m) wide and 5 inches (130&#160;mm) thick.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smaller_Dining_or_Breakfast_Room">Smaller Dining or Breakfast Room</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Smaller Dining or Breakfast Room">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Above Taylor\'s vaulted kitchen, between the Pillared Room and the State Dining room, Soane created a Smaller Dining Room (sometimes called the Breakfast Room) that still exists. To build it, Soane removed the chimney from the kitchen to put a door in the room. He then moved the chimney to the east side, running a Y-shaped split flue inside the walls up either side of one of the windows above. The room therefore has a unique architectural feature: over the fireplace there is a window instead of the usual <a href="/wiki/Chimney_breast" title="Chimney breast">chimney breast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_179_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_179-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With its flat unadorned ceiling, simple mouldings and deep window seats, the Small Dining Room is intimate and comfortable. Usually furnished with a mahogany table seating only eight, Prime Ministers have often used this room when dining with family or when entertaining special guests on more personal state occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <i>(See the Small Dining or Breakfast Room c1927. The double doors behind the table lead to the State Dining Room.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>)</i><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrace_and_garden">Terrace and garden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Terrace and garden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ImperialConference1923.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ImperialConference1923.jpg/220px-ImperialConference1923.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ImperialConference1923.jpg/330px-ImperialConference1923.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ImperialConference1923.jpg/440px-ImperialConference1923.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="469" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ImperialConference1923.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister Stanley Baldwin (seated centre with his legs crossed) poses in the garden with representatives to the Imperial Conference \xe2\x80\x93 1923.</div></div></div>\n<p>The terrace and garden were constructed in 1736 shortly after Walpole moved into Number 10. The terrace, extending across the back, provides a full view of St James\'s Park. The garden is dominated by an open lawn of 0.5 acres (2,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) that wraps around Numbers 10 and 11 in an L-shape. No longer "fitted with variety Walle fruit and diverse fruit trees" as it was in the 17th century, there is now a centrally located flower bed around a holly tree surrounded by seats. Tubs of flowers line the steps from the terrace; around the walls are rose beds with flowering and evergreen shrubs.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_180_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_180-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> (See North elevation of Number 10 with steps leading to the garden<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The terrace and garden have provided a casual setting for many gatherings of First Lords with foreign dignitaries, Cabinet ministers, guests, and staff. Prime Minister Tony Blair, for example, hosted a farewell reception in 2007 for his staff on the terrace. John Major announced his 1995 resignation as leader of the Conservative Party in the garden. Churchill called his secretaries the "garden girls" because their offices overlook the garden.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> It was also the location of the first press conference announcing the Coalition Government between <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Furnishings">Furnishings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Furnishings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Number 10 is filled with fine paintings, sculptures, busts and furniture. Only a few are permanent features. Most are on loan. About half belong to the Government Art Collection. The remainder are on loan from private collectors and from public galleries such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery,_London" title="National Portrait Gallery, London">National Portrait Gallery</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tate_Gallery" class="mw-redirect" title="Tate Gallery">Tate Gallery</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery" title="National Gallery">National Gallery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> (See works from the Government Art Collection currently on display at Number 10<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>)\n</p><p>About a dozen paintings are changed annually. More extensive changes occur when a new Prime Minister takes office and redecorates.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> These redecorations may reflect both individual taste as well as make a political statement. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> borrowed French paintings from the National Gallery and was loaned two Renoirs from a private collector. When Margaret Thatcher arrived in 1979 she insisted that the artwork had to be British and that it celebrate "British achievers". As a former chemist, she took pleasure in devoting the Small Dining Room to a collection of portraits of British scientists, such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>. During the 1990s <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> converted the first floor anteroom into a small gallery of modern art, mostly British. He also introduced several paintings by <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">John Constable</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a>, Britain\'s two best known 19th-century artists, and cricketing paintings by <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Stuart-Wortley_(painter)" title="Archibald Stuart-Wortley (painter)">Archibald Stuart-Wortley</a> including a portrait of one of England\'s most celebrated batsmen <a href="/wiki/W._G._Grace" title="W. G. Grace">W. G. Grace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to outstanding artwork, Number 10 contains many exceptional pieces of furniture either owned by the house or on loan. One of the most striking and unusual is the Chippendale hooded guard\'s chair already mentioned that sits in a corner of the entrance hall. To its left is a long case clock by Benson of Whitehaven. A similar clock by Samuel Whichcote of London stands in the Cabinet anteroom. The White State Drawing Room contains elegant Adams furniture. The Green State Drawing Room contains mostly Chippendale furniture including a card table that belonged to Clive of India and a mahogany desk that is thought to have belonged to William Pitt the Younger and used by him during the Napoleonic Wars. In addition to the large carpet previously described, the Pillared State Drawing Room also contains a marble-topped table by Kent. The State Dining Room contains an elegant mahogany <a href="/wiki/Sideboard" title="Sideboard">sideboard</a> by Adam.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="250th_anniversary:_1985">250th anniversary: 1985</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 250th anniversary: 1985">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1985, Number 10 was 250 years old. To celebrate, Thatcher hosted a grand dinner in the State Dining Room for her living predecessors\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Also in attendance were <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> and representatives of the families of every 20th-century Prime Minister since <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">Asquith</a>, including Lady Olwen Carey Evans (daughter of <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyd George">Lloyd George</a>), Lady Leonora Howard (daughter of <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Clarissa_Eden,_Countess_of_Avon" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarissa Eden, Countess of Avon">Clarissa Avon</a> (widow of <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a> and niece of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>That same year, the Leisure Circle published Christopher Jones\' book <i>No. 10 Downing Street, The Story of a House</i>. In the foreword,  Thatcher described her feelings for Number 10: "How much I wish that the public&#160;... could share with me the feeling of Britain\'s historic greatness which pervades every nook and cranny of this complicated and meandering old building&#160;... All Prime Ministers are intensely aware that, as tenants and stewards of No. 10 Downing Street, they have in their charge one of the most precious jewels in the nation\'s heritage".<sup id="cite_ref-multiref1_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multiref1-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_after_the_1991_bombing">Security after the 1991 bombing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Security after the 1991 bombing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_mortar_attack" title="Downing Street mortar attack">Downing Street mortar attack</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Downing_Street_gates.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Downing_Street_gates.jpg/220px-Downing_Street_gates.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Downing_Street_gates.jpg/330px-Downing_Street_gates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Downing_Street_gates.jpg/440px-Downing_Street_gates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Downing_Street_gates.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gates at the main entrance of Downing Street</div></div></div>\n<p>For most of its history, Number 10 was accessible to the public. Early security consisted of two police officers.  One stood guard outside the door.  The other was stationed inside to open it. Since the door had no keyhole, the inside officer depended upon the lone outside officer.\n</p><p>During Thatcher\'s premiership, terrorist threats led to the implementation of a second level of security. Guarded gates were added at both ends of the street. Visitors could then be screened before approaching the door.\n</p><p>Despite the added measure,  on 7 February 1991 the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA">Provisional IRA</a> used a van they parked in <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a> to launch a <a href="/wiki/Mortar_shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortar shell">mortar shell</a> at Number 10. It exploded in the back garden, while then prime minister <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> was holding a Cabinet meeting. Major moved to <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_House_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Admiralty House (London)">Admiralty House</a> while repairs were completed. This led to the addition of guardhouses at the street ends as well as other less visible measures. Each <a href="/wiki/Guardhouse" title="Guardhouse">guardhouse</a> is staffed by several armed police officers.\nThe Metropolitan Police Service\'s <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_Protection_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Diplomatic Protection Group">Diplomatic Protection Group</a> (DPG) provides overall protection and acts on intelligence from <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Prime_Minister.27s_Office"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister\'s_Office">Prime Minister\'s Office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Prime Minister&#039;s Office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The Prime Minister\'s Office, for which the terms <i>Downing Street</i> and <i>Number 10</i> are synonymous, lies within the 10 Downing Street building and is part of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Office" title="Cabinet Office">Cabinet Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CO_0001_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CO_0001-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> It is staffed by a mix of career <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Civil_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Civil Service">Civil Servants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_advisers_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Special advisers in the United Kingdom">Special Advisers</a>. The highest ranking Civil Servant position is the <a href="/wiki/Principal_Private_Secretary_to_the_Prime_Minister" title="Principal Private Secretary to the Prime Minister">Principal Private Secretary to the Prime Minister</a>, currently<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> Peter Hill; the most senior Special Adviser post is the <a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_Chief_of_Staff" title="Downing Street Chief of Staff">Downing Street Chief of Staff</a>, currently<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> held by <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Barwell" title="Gavin Barwell">Gavin Barwell</a>. Though Number 10 is formally part of the Cabinet Office, it reports directly to the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Secretary_(United_Kingdom)" title="Cabinet Secretary (United Kingdom)">Cabinet Secretary</a>, who is currently<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Heywood" title="Jeremy Heywood">Sir Jeremy Heywood</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_positions_within_the_Office_of_the_Prime_Minister">Current positions within the Office of the Prime Minister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Current positions within the Office of the Prime Minister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>List of current Civil Servant and Special Adviser positions appointed by the current Prime Minister Theresa May:</b>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Position\n</th>\n<th>Current holder\n</th>\n<th>Term started\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_Chief_of_Staff" title="Downing Street Chief of Staff">Downing Street Chief of Staff</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Gavin_Barwell" title="Gavin Barwell">Gavin Barwell</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>10 June 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Downing Street Head of Operations\n</td>\n<td><b>Richard Jackson MBE</b>\n</td>\n<td>16 September 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Principal_Private_Secretary_to_the_Prime_Minister" title="Principal Private Secretary to the Prime Minister">Principal Private Secretary to the Prime Minister</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Peter_Hill_(civil_servant)" title="Peter Hill (civil servant)">Peter Hill</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>10 May 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Private_Secretary_to_the_Prime_Minister" title="Parliamentary Private Secretary to the Prime Minister">Parliamentary Private Secretary to the Prime Minister</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/George_Hollingbery" title="George Hollingbery">George Hollingbery</a></b> <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MP</a></span>\n</td>\n<td>17 July 2016\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Seema_Kennedy" title="Seema Kennedy">Seema Kennedy</a></b> <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MP</a></span>\n</td>\n<td>28 June 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_Director_of_Communications" title="Downing Street Director of Communications">Downing Street Director of Communications</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Robbie_Gibb" title="Robbie Gibb">Robbie Gibb</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>6 July 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Downing_Street_Press_Secretary" title="Downing Street Press Secretary">Downing Street Press Secretary</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/James_Slack" title="James Slack">James Slack</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>18 April 2017\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Number_10_Policy_Unit" title="Number 10 Policy Unit">Downing Street Director of Policy</a>\n</td>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/James_Marshall_(political_adviser)" title="James Marshall (political adviser)">James Marshall</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>27 June 2017\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<ul><li>On 10 June 2017, the joint Downing Street Chief of Staffs were sacked by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> over concerns she would face a leadership challenge following the result of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election</a>. Later the same day, the Prime Minister appointed <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Barwell" title="Gavin Barwell">Gavin Barwell</a> as the Downing Street Chief of Staff.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Downing Street Director of Communications and Press Secretary resigned simultaneously on 18 April 2017 over disagreement with how the  office was run.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister%27s_Official_Spokesperson" title="Prime Minister&#39;s Official Spokesperson">Prime Minister\'s Official Spokesperson</a> assumed the position of Downing Street Press Secretary on the same day, and <a href="/wiki/Robbie_Gibb" title="Robbie Gibb">Robbie Gibb</a> was appointed as the new Downing Street Director of Communications by the Prime Minister on 6 July 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Downing Street Director of Policy resigned on 21 June 2017, also as a result following the 2017 general election.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The Prime Minister appointed <a href="/wiki/James_Marshall_(political_adviser)" title="James Marshall (political adviser)">James Marshall</a> as the new Director of Policy a week later on 27 June 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>On 28 June 2017, <a href="/wiki/Seema_Kennedy" title="Seema Kennedy">Seema Kennedy</a> was appointed as Parliamentary Private Secretary to the Prime Minister, a position which she shares with <a href="/wiki/George_Hollingbery" title="George Hollingbery">George Hollingbery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Structure_of_the_Prime_Minister.27s_Office"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_of_the_Prime_Minister\'s_Office">Structure of the Prime Minister\'s Office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Structure of the Prime Minister&#039;s Office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em">Pre-2001 organisation</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;text-align:left">\n<ul><li>The No. 10 Private Office (government coordination, diary management and correspondence);</li>\n<li>The No. 10 Press Office (press and public relations) \xe2\x80\x93 The press office has grown in significance as media attention on the PM has intensified. Thatcher\'s press secretary <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Ingham" title="Bernard Ingham">Bernard Ingham</a> was one of her most important advisors. <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Campbell" title="Alastair Campbell">Alastair Campbell</a>\'s influence as Blair\'s press secretary was even greater;</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/No._10_Policy_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="No. 10 Policy Unit">No. 10 Policy Unit</a> (policy review and advice);</li>\n<li>The No. 10 Political Office (party political liaison and constituency affairs);</li></ul></td>\n</tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The office was reorganised in 2001 into 3 directorates:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Policy and government</b>\n<dl><dd>Took over the functions of the private office and policy unit. Prepares advice for the PM and coordinates development and implementation of policy across departments.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><b>Communication and strategy</b>\n<ul><li>Press office: responsible for relations with the media</li>\n<li>Direct communications unit</li>\n<li>Research and information unit: provides factual information to No. 10</li>\n<li>Strategy unit</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Government and political relations</b>\n<dl><dd>Handles party and constituency affairs</dd></dl></li></ul>\n<p>Changes were intended to strengthen the PM\'s office. However, some commentators have suggested that Blair\'s reforms have created something similar to a \'Prime Ministers\' department.<sup id="cite_ref-PM&#39;s_department_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PM&#39;s_department-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The reorganisation brought about the fusion of the old Prime Minister\'s Office and other Cabinet Office teams, with a number of units (including the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister%27s_Strategy_Unit" title="Prime Minister&#39;s Strategy Unit">Prime Minister\'s Strategy Unit</a>) now reporting directly into the Prime Minister\'s Office. Since 2005, Number 10s Direct Communication Unit has not used its staff\'s real names on signed correspondence to MPs and members of the public; this is for security reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Government" title="Institute for Government">Institute for Government</a> has written that the Cabinet Office (of which the Prime Minister\'s Office is a component) "is a long way from becoming a fully fledged premier\'s department", primarily based on the fact that the Prime Minister "largely lacks the direct policy responsibilities, either in statute or by convention under the Royal Prerogative, possessed by secretaries of state, who have substantial budgets voted to them by Parliament."<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=10_Downing_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_residents_of_10_Downing_Street" title="List of residents of 10 Downing Street">List of residents of 10 Downing Street</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chequers" title="Chequers">Chequers</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the Prime Minister\'s official country residence</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Larry_(cat)" title="Larry (cat)">Larry</a> \xe2\x80\x93 a cat employed as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Mouser_to_the_Cabinet_Office" title="Chief Mouser to the Cabinet Office">Chief Mouser to the Cabinet Office</a> at 10 Downing Street</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street_Guard_Chairs" title="10 Downing Street Guard Chairs">10 Downing Street Guard Chairs</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Official_residence" title="Official residence">Official residence</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 October</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Architecture+of+England%2C+Scotland%2C+and+Wales&amp;rft.pages=187&amp;rft.pub=Greenwood+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-313-31850-4&amp;rft.aulast=Jones&amp;rft.aufirst=Nigel+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DepsFOeV1mCMC%26pg%3DPA187&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3A10+Downing+Street" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, p. 33.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See letter, dated, "Downing Street, 30 June 1742", from <a href="/wiki/Horace_Walpole" title="Horace Walpole">Horace Walpole</a> to Sir <a href="/wiki/Horace_Mann" title="Horace Mann">Horace Mann</a>: "I am writing to you in one of the charming rooms towards the Park: it is I am willing to enjoy this sweet corner while I may, for we are soon to quit it. Mrs. Sandys came yesterday to give us warning; <a href="/wiki/Lord_Wilmington" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Wilmington">Lord Wilmington</a> has lent it to them. <i>Sir Robert might have had it for his own at first: but would only take it as First Lord of the Treasury.</i> He goes into a small house of his own in Arlington Street, opposite to where we formerly lived". (Horace Walpole\'s Letters, ed. Cunningham, 1857, I, p. 246.) British History Online, From: \'<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.british-history.ac.uk/report.aspx?compid=67934">No. 10, Downing Street</a>\', Survey of London: volume 14: St Margaret, Westminster, part III: Whitehall II (1931), pp. 113\xe2\x80\x93141. Date accessed: 21 July 2008.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Feely, p. 34.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bolitho, p. 25.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, p. 50.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-multiref6-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-multiref6_34-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-multiref6_34-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Seldon, p. 16.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Jones-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Jones_35-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Jones_35-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones, p. 46.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Miney, p. 47.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, pp. 46\xe2\x80\x9347.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Jones,_p.51-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Jones,_p.51_38-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Jones,_p.51_38-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones, p. 51.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, p. 29.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones, p. 52. Henry Pelham, for example, had his own spacious home and had no need for Number 10. In a piece of "blatant political corruption", he allowed his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pelham-Clinton,_2nd_Duke_of_Newcastle-under-Lyne" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Pelham-Clinton, 2nd Duke of Newcastle-under-Lyne">Henry Clinton</a>, Earl of Lincoln, to live there from 1745 to 1753 even though Clinton was not involved in politics.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, pp. 173 and 179. <a href="/wiki/Lord_Liverpool" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a> assigned it to his two Chancellors of the Exchequer, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Vansittart" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicholas Vansittart">Nicholas Vansittart</a> (1812\xe2\x80\x931823) and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Robinson,_1st_Viscount_Goderich" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Robinson, 1st Viscount Goderich">Frederick Robinson</a> (1823\xe2\x80\x931827).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">British History Online, Letter (B.M. Addl. MS. 38292, f. 11) from Lord Liverpool to Charles Ellis, dated 22 January 1823, is of interest. "When you spoke to me some time ago upon the subject of the House in Downing Street, I was under the impression, as you were yourself, that the house was the King\'s &amp; that he might dispose of it in any manner he might think proper. <i>Upon Inquiry, however, it appeared that the House was attached to the Treasury as a Part of the Office. That the First Lord of the Treasury occupies it if he thinks proper. If he declines it, the Chancellor of the Exchequer occupies it, not as Chancellor of the Exchequer, but as second in the Commission of the Treasury. That if he declined it, it would go to the next in the Commission, or it might possibly be disposed of by the Board to any Member or Officer of the Treasury; but could not, &amp; never has been detached from it. You are mistaken in supposing that Mr. Vansittart is the only Chancellor of the Exchequer who, without being first Lord of the Treasury, occupied it.</i> Lord North certainly occupied it during the two years he was Chancellor of the Exchequer only. I believe Mr. Charles Townshend occupied it, but I know Mr. Dowdeswell did, &amp; it is remarkable that he is, I believe, the only instance of a Chancellor of the Exchequer upon Record who was not in the Cabinet. The House stands in fact upon the same footing as the Houses of the Admiralty, which could not be assigned to any Person not belonging to that office".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bolitho, p. 116. A few peers lived in Number 10 out of necessity. The Duke of Wellington, for example, grudgingly lived there for eighteen months between 1828 and 1830 because his own home, Apsley House, was undergoing extensive renovations. He left as soon as it was finished.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the end of the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> lived in his town house on Arlington Street. During his last ministry from 1895 to 1902, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, his nephew lived in Number 10. Minney, p. 322.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, pp. 83\xe2\x80\x9384.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-multiref7-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-multiref7_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-multiref7_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, p. 117.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.british-history.ac.uk/image.aspx?compid=67934&amp;filename=fig52.gif&amp;pubid=748">fig52</a>, british-history.ac.uk</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-multiref8-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-multiref8_48-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-multiref8_48-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones, p. 71.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jones, p. 72.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.british-history.ac.uk/image.aspx?compid=68051&amp;filename=figure0748-111.gif&amp;pubid=748">figure0748-111</a>, british-history.ac.uk</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">British History Online, From: \'<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.british-history.ac.uk/report.aspx?compid=68051">Plate 111: No. 10, Downing Street: plan of alterations in 1781</a>\', Survey of London: volume 14: St Margaret, Westminster, part III: Whitehall II (1931), pp. 111. 138.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a rel="nofollow" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=BBC+News&amp;rft.atitle=Welcome+to+the+Dave+and+Nick+Show&amp;rft.date=2010-05-12&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fnews.bbc.co.uk%2F1%2Fhi%2Fuk_politics%2Felection_2010%2F8678370.stm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3A10+Downing+Street" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seldon, p. 172.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.gac.culture.gov.uk/location_results.aspx?Place=1008&amp;lt=thumbnails&amp;sb=ArtistName&amp;tl=1008,1065,1070,1195,1140&amp;pg=1">Government Art Collection</a> gac.culture.gov.uk</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sheldon. p. 174.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seldon, pp. 172\xe2\x80\x93173.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-139"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-139">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seldon, pp. 185\xe2\x80\x93188.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Until the late 19th century, Prime Ministers were required to furnish Number 10 at their own expense with furniture, tableware, china, linens, curtains and decorations. This arrangement began to change in 1877 when Benjamin Disraeli took up residency. He insisted that the Treasury should bear the cost of furnishings at least in the public areas. The Treasury agreed and a complex accounting procedure was developed whereby the outgoing Prime Minister was required to pay for "wear and tear" on furnishings that had been purchased by the Treasury. This system was used until November 1897 when the Treasury assumed responsibility for purchasing and maintaining almost all of the furnishings in both the public and private areas except decorating the walls with art work. In 1924 when Prime Minister Ramsay MacDonald took office, he did not own nor have the means to buy an extensive art collection. He had the Government Art Collection loan pieces. The arrangement became the standard practice. Minney, p 285\xe2\x80\x93286</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Time Magazine, <i>People</i> by Ellie McGrath, 16 December 1985</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seldon, p. 90.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-CO_0001-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CO_0001_143-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100701163002/http://www.cabinetoffice.gov.uk/about-cabinet-office/cabinet-office-structure.aspx">"Cabinet Office structure"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Office" title="Cabinet Office">Cabinet Office</a> HM Government. 10 June 2010.
